Resolving the Implication of Command and Prohibition for Opposites
Resolves a logical paradox regarding command and prohibition of opposites using Hanafi usul principles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi principle that a command implies prohibition of its opposite only under specific conditions (muḍayyaq/musa‘a) and apply the concept of aspects (jihaat) to resolve apparent contradictions between obligation and prohibition.

Core Idea
A command prohibits its opposite only when the obligation is narrow in time; otherwise, both can coexist under different aspects.

In Hanafi usul al-fiqh, a common objection arises: if a command prohibits its opposite, then applying this recursively leads to contradictions. This passage from Fawatih al-Rahamut addresses this by explaining that obligation and prohibition can coexist in the same act under different aspects (jihaat). More importantly, it specifies that the prohibition of the opposite only applies when the obligation is narrow in time (muḍayyaq). If the obligation is broad (musa‘a), the opposite may be permissible at different times.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
State the logical objection: if command A prohibits its opposite B, then B's prohibition commands its opposite C, making C both prohibited (as opposite to A) and commanded (as opposite to B), leading to contradiction.
This is the initial 'if you say...' part of the passage.
2
Understand the Hanafi response using aspects (jihaat)
Explain that obligation and prohibition can coexist in the same act when considered from different perspectives (jihaat). For example, prayer during Hajj is forbidden as it nullifies the current performance of Hajj, but it remains obligatory as a daily prayer.
The passage uses the phrase 'al-inkān bi-l-naẓar ilā shayʾ lā yunāfī al-imtināʿ bi-l-naẓar ilā shayʾ ākhar'.
3
Apply the muḍayyaq/musa‘a distinction
Recognize that the prohibition of the opposite only holds when the obligation is narrow in time (muḍayyaq). For broad obligations (musa‘a), the opposite can be performed at another time, so the prohibition is not absolute.
The passage states: 'al-sharṭ fī ḥurmat ḍidd al-wājib an yakūn al-wājib muḍayyaqan'.
4
Analyze the example of Hajj and prayer
Apply the distinction to the example: Hajj is a musa‘a obligation (the entire lifetime), so performing prayer during Hajj is only forbidden if it nullifies the current performance of Hajj, not absolutely. The prohibition is limited to the aspect of nullification.
The passage addresses the objection about the consensus that Hajj's time is the entire life.
5
Evaluate the objection of consensus
Reconcile the consensus with the principle: the entire lifetime is the time for Hajj only considering the obligation itself, not the prohibition of its opposite at every moment. The prohibition is confined to the part of time where the opposite nullifies the obligation.
The passage ends with 'illā an yuqāl ... naẓaran ilayhi min ḥaythu huwa huwa'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إن قلت : فالأمر بشيء نهي عن ضده عيناً) الأضداد عنه (والنهي عن الضد يستلزم الأمر بالضد الآخر تخييراً، فهذا الضد) الآخر (منهي عنه عيناً ومأمور به تخييراً فاجتمع الوجوب والحرمة في شيء واحد (هذا خلف قلت الإمكان بالنظر إلى شيء كما أنه لا ينافي الامتناع بالذات ولا الامتناع بالنظر إلى شيء آخر) كذلك المأمور به والوجوب بالنظر إلى شيء لا ينافي الحرمة بالنظر إلى شيء آخر فلا استحالة في الاجتماع ولو قيل : إن حرمة ضد الواجب لأنه مفوّت له، وليس الاجتناب عنه مطلوباً بالذات بل لأداء الواجب وضد المنهي إنما يكون واجباً ليحصل الاجتناب عنه فضد هذا الضد إنما يكون مطلوباً ليحصل الاجتناب عن هذا الضد، وإنما هو لتحصيل الواجب، فإن كان ضد الضد ضداً للواجب أيضاً لا يكون واجباً مطلوباً للاجتناب عن هذا الضد، لأن الاجتناب عنه على هذا الوجه لم يكن مطلوباً بل بوجه يقارنه أداء الواجب لم يكن بعيداً، بل أولى كما لا يخفى على المتأمل (لا يقال : يلزم على (الأول هو تضمن وجوب الشيء حرمة الضد (حرمة الواجبات) فإن من الواجبات ما هو ضد الواجب آخر (كحرمة الصلاة من حيث أنها ضد (الحج) إذ الأركان الصلاتية لا تجامع الأركان الحجية وبالعكس) أي حرمة الحج من حيث إنه ضد الصلاة (و) يلزم على الثاني) وهو تضمن نهي الشيء وجوب الضد (وجوب المحرمات ولو تخييراً فإن من المحرمات ما هو ضد لمحرم آخر (كوجوب الزنا لأنه ترك اللواطة إذ الإيلاجان لا يجتمعان (وبالعكس) أي وجوب اللواطة لأنه ترك الزنا (لأنا نقول في الأول) أي لأجل الجواب عن الأول الأمر لا يقتضي الاستيعاب فلا يكون نهياً الضد دائما) بل في هذه الأحيان ولا شناعة في الالتزام فأداء الصلاة بنحو يكون الحج بها متروكاً حرام البتة (فيمكن فعل ضده الواجب في وقت آخر ومن ها هنا قيل : إن الشرط) في حرمة ضد الواجب أن يكون الواجب مضيقاً فإن الموسع لا يوجب حرمة الضد، إذ يجوز تركه، والأصح أنه لا حاجة إلى هذا التقييد، إذ كما أنه يجب الموسع في جزء من أجزاء الوقت، كذلك الاشتغال بضده أو أضداده فيه، فإن الحرمة على حسب الوجوب (لكن يلزم) على هذا (أن لا يكون الحج وقته العمر كله فإنه حرام في جزء من أجزاء الوقت وقد أجمع على أن العمر كله وقته، وكذا يلزم في الصلاة أيضاً أن لا يكون تمام وقته المقدر وقتاً له (إلا أن يقال) في الجواب (ذلك) أي العمر كله وقته) نظرا إليه من حيث هو هو) حتى يكون أداء الحج في كل
عن
يحرم
If you say: 'The command for a thing is a prohibition of its opposite exactly, and the prohibition of the opposite necessitates the command of the other opposite by way of choice. So this other opposite is prohibited exactly and commanded by choice. Thus obligation and prohibition meet in one thing. This is impossible.' I say: 'The possibility with respect to one thing does not negate impossibility by essence nor impossibility with respect to another thing.' Similarly, the commanded and the obligation with respect to one thing does not negate the prohibition with respect to another thing. So there is no impossibility in combination. And if it is said: 'The prohibition of the opposite of the obligatory is because it nullifies it, and avoiding it is not desired essentially but for performing the obligatory. The opposite of the forbidden is only obligatory to achieve avoidance of it. So the opposite of this opposite is only desired to achieve avoidance of this opposite, and it is only for accomplishing the obligatory. If the opposite of the opposite is also an opposite of the obligatory, it is not obligatory as desired for avoidance of this opposite, because avoidance of it in this manner was not desired, but in a manner that accompanies the performance of the obligatory was not far-fetched, rather more appropriate, as is not hidden from the contemplator.' (It cannot be said: 'Necessary on the first (that obligation of a thing implies prohibition of its opposite) is the prohibition of obligations, for among obligations is what is opposite to another obligation, like the prohibition of prayer insofar as it is opposite to Hajj, since the pillars of prayer do not coexist with the pillars of Hajj and vice versa. And necessary on the second (that prohibition of a thing implies obligation of its opposite) is the obligation of forbidden things by choice, for among forbidden things is what is opposite to another forbidden thing, like the obligation of zina because it is abandoning liwat, since the two insertions do not coexist, and vice versa.) For we say regarding the first: The command does not require continuity, so it is not a prohibition of the opposite always, but at times. And there is no shame in adhering to it. So performing prayer in a manner that leaves Hajj is absolutely forbidden. (And it is possible to perform its opposite obligatory at another time. And from here it is said: The condition for the prohibition of the opposite of the obligatory is that the obligatory be narrow in time; the broad obligatory does not necessitate the prohibition of its opposite, as it may be abandoned. The more correct view is that there is no need for this restriction, for just as the broad obligatory is required in a part of the time, so is engagement with its opposite or opposites in it. So the prohibition is according to the obligation. (But this necessitates that Hajj is not the entire life as its time, for it is forbidden in a part of the time, and there is consensus that the entire life is its time. Similarly for prayer: that the entire estimated time is not its time. (Unless it is said in answer: That (i.e., the entire life as its time) is considered in itself, so that performing Hajj in every... towards... forbidden.)
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage addresses a logical objection to the rule that a command implies prohibition of its opposite. The objector argues that if A is commanded, then its opposite B is prohibited; but then B's opposite C (which is commanded by the prohibition of B) would be both prohibited (as opposite to A) and commanded (as opposite to B), leading to a contradiction. The response clarifies that obligation and prohibition can coexist in one act under different aspects (jihaat). For example, performing a prayer during the time of Hajj may be forbidden under one aspect (as it leaves Hajj) but may be neutral or permissible under another aspect. The passage further discusses the condition that the obligatory act must be narrow in time (muḍayyaq) for its opposite to be forbidden; if the time is broad (musa‘a), the opposite may not be forbidden because it can be performed later. The example of Hajj and prayer is used: since Hajj is a broad obligation (the entire life), performing prayer during Hajj does not make prayer forbidden absolutely, but rather the prohibition is only with respect to the current moment if it nullifies the current performance of Hajj. The resolution is that the prohibition of the opposite is not continuous but depends on the time and aspect.

Key Terms
مضيق (muḍayyaq): A narrow-time obligation that must be performed within a specific time frame, causing its opposite to be forbidden
موسع (musa‘a): A broad-time obligation that can be performed at any time, thus its opposite is not necessarily forbidden
جهة (jiha): Aspect or perspective from which a ruling applies

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi view, why is it possible for an act to be both obligatory and forbidden?
A) Because the act can be performed at different times
B) Because the rulings apply under different aspects (jihaat)
C) Because one ruling overrides the other
D) Because the act is inherently neutral

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi distinction between muḍayyaq and musa‘a obligations resolves the apparent contradiction that a command can imply prohibition of its opposite only conditionally, not absolutely.

Command and Prohibition of Opposites: A Hanafi Analysis
Analyzing whether a command implies prohibition of its opposite

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position that a command does not entail prohibition of its opposite, identify the opposing arguments (identity and implication), and explain the practical legal consequence (thamarah al-khilaf).

Core Idea
A command to do X does not automatically prohibit its opposite; they are independent legal categories.

In Hanafi usul, the relationship between a command (amr) and the prohibition of its opposite (nahy 'an al-didd) is a classic point of dispute. This passage from Fawatih al-Rahamut presents the Hanafi refutation of two extreme views: that the command is identical to the prohibition of its opposite, or that it necessarily implies it. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correct derivation of rulings and preventing overly broad prohibitions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three positions
Recognize the three views: (1) identity (al-'ayniyya) – the command is the prohibition; (2) implication (al-tadammun) – the command entails prohibition; (3) Hanafi position – no entailment.
The passage refutes the first two.
2
Examine the opposing arguments
Analyze al-Qadi's arguments: first, without identity/implication, command and prohibition would conflict; second, stillness as abandonment of motion shows identity.
Note the retraction from identity to implication.
3
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi reply: commands and prohibitions are different; stillness is outside the dispute. The opposite is a necessary concomitant, not the object of command. Conception of specific opposites is not required.
Key: command only requires general conception of opposition.
4
Derive the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: a command to pray does not make eating (its opposite) prohibited. Separate evidence is needed for prohibition.
This limits the scope of prohibition to explicit texts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقت صحيحاً، ولا تبقى المؤاخذة به وإنما جاء الحرمة في بعض الأحيان نظراً إلى ترك واجب آخر (و) نقول في الثاني (التعيين للحرمة لدليل (أصلي) موجب له (أخرج المحل عن قبول التخيير تبعاً فإن الحكيم ليس شأنه أن يأمر بشيء ليأمن عن الحرام وهو حرام مثله، فلا يكون الكف عن الزنا مطلقاً ولو باللواطة مطلوباً بل الكف الخاص، فلا يلزم وجوب اللواطة فتأمل فيه، ويمكن الجواب بعدم التنافي بين الوجوب التبعي تخييراً والحرمة الذاتية فتدبر (ولأصحاب سائر المذاهب وجوه ضعيفة مذكورة في المبسوطات مع ما عليها فارجع إليها) ونحن نذكرها فاعلم أن القائلين بالعينية، قال القاضي منهم : أولاً : لو لم يكن الأمر بشيء هو النهي عن الضد فهما إما مثلان أو ضدان أو خلافان، وعلى الأولين يلزم أن لا يصح الاجتماع، ويصح بالضرورة إذ لا استحالة في الأمر بشيء والنهي عن ضده وبالعكس، وعلى الثالث فيمكن اجتماع الأمر بالشيء مع ضد النهي عن الضد وضده أمر به، فيلزم اجتماع الأمر بالشيء مع الأمر بضده هذا خلف قلنا خلافان، ولا نسلم لزوم إمكان الأمر مر مع ضد النهي عن الضد، فإنه يجوز التلازم بين الأمر والنهي عن الضد، فلا يصح الانفكاك، نعم يلزم التضمن كما علمت ولعله لهذا رجع القاضي عنها إلى التضمن، وثانياً: إن السكون ترك الحركة، فالأمر بالسكون طلب لترك الحركة وهو النهي عن الحركة قلنا: الأضداد التي هي سلوب المأمورات مسلم أنه عين تركها لكنها خارجة عن النزاع، فإنه في الأضداد الثابتة الجزئية، وأما كون كل ضد مأمور به تركاً له فممنوع كيف وليس الأكل نفس ترك الصلاة، نعم ترك الضد من لوازم وجود المأمور به فالأمر به ملزوم النهي عن الضد، وظن المخصص العينية أو التضمن بالأمر إما أن النهي لا يقتضي إلا نفي الفعل وليس وجود الضد عينه أو لازم لجواز انتفاء الفعل بانتفاء المقتضى لا لوجود المانع ، وقد مر أنه وارد، وأما لزوم وجوب المحرمات، وقد مر الجواب عنه، وأما لزوم انتفاء المباح ، وسيجيء إن شاء الله تعالى حاله، فظن المخصص بأمر الوجوب أحد الأمرين الأخيرين إذ ما من وقت إلا وفيه مندوب، فيلزم أن يكون كل مباح مكروهاً، وسيجيء إن شاء الله تعالى ما ينكشف به حاله، المنكرون للعينية والتضمن قالوا: لو كان الأمر بشيء هو بعينه النهي عن الضد أو ملزوماً له وبالعكس، من الأمر بشيء والنهي عن شيء تعقل الأضداد والتالي باطل بالضرورة، أما الملازمة فلأنه لزم، لا يعقل أمر ولا نهي من غير تعقل متعلقهما قلنا : لزوم التعقل فيما يكون التكليف به بأمر أو نهي صريحاً أو لازماً بيناً لتكليف صريح وليس الأمر فيما نحن فيه، كذلك فإن النهي عن الضد لازم للأمر لزوماً غير بين وإن ادعى أنه بين بالمعنى الأعم، وأجيب في المشهور بأن المنفي تعقل ضد ضد وأما تعقل مطلق الضد فضروري، لأن الأمر لا يكون إلا حال انعدام المأمور به وإلا لزم طلب الحاصل، وعدمه لا يكون إلا باشتغال الضد فلزم تعقل الضد المطلق، والحاصل أن تعقل الأضداد الجزئية على التفصيل غير ضروري وعدمه مسلم، وأما تعقلها بالوجه الأعم كالضدية فلازم ضروري ومتحقق ها هنا فلا يرد أنه إذا سلم انتفاء تعقل ضد ضد فقد سلم ما ادعى المستدل فإن الكلام في أن الأضداد الجزئية منهي عنها أم لا كما في «التحرير»، ولا يرد أيضاً أنه سلم أولاً انتفاء تعقل الأضداد الجزئية، وآخراً أثبت تعقل ضد ما كما في «التحرير» أيضاً، واعترض على هذا الجواب أولاً بأنه لا يلزم انتفاء المأمور به حال الأمر، بل غاية ما يلزم انتفاء المأمور به في الاستقبال فلا يلزم تعقل الضد وهذا غير واف فإن للمجيب أن يقول لا بد من تعقل انتفاء المأمور به في الاستقبال والاشتغال بضد، وبهذا القدر يتم المطلوب، فالأحرى في الاعتراض عليه بأن الأمر لا يقتضي تعقل الانتفاء ولو في الاستقبال، ألا ترى أن المطيع مأمور من الله تعالى وعلمه محيط بكل شيء وكذا لا يلزم الانتفاء حال الأمر، فإن المؤمن مأمور بالإيمان في الاستقبال بل لا بد من تعقل أنه غير حاصل من غير صنع المأمور، ويمكن انتفاؤه بمشيئة، وهو لا يستلزم تعقل الضد أصلاً، وثانياً : بأن غاية ما لزم تعقل الضد ولم يكن المستدل نفاه بل نفي تعقل الضد منهياً أو مطلوباً، فإن مقصوده لو كان الأمر نفس النهي عن الضد أو ملزومه وبالعكس لزم تعقل الأضداد في الأمر منهية وفي النهي مأمورة، إذ الأمر والنهي بشيء لا يعقل من غير تعقله بهذا النحو من التعقل والحق في الجواب ما ذكرنا، ولقد وقع ها هنا نوع من الإطناب وبعد بقي خبايا وعليه التكلان.
And [the command] is considered valid, and retribution does not remain with it; rather, prohibition came in some instances due to abandoning another obligatory act. And we say in the second [issue]: specification of prohibition due to an original evidence obligating it removed the locus from accepting choice incidentally, for the Wise is not such that He commands something so that one may be safe from the unlawful while it is itself unlawful. Thus, abstention from adultery absolutely, even by sodomy, is not demanded, but rather specific abstention. So the obligation of sodomy does not follow; reflect on it. And it is possible to answer that there is no contradiction between incidental obligatory choice and intrinsic prohibition; so ponder. And for the adherents of other schools there are weak positions mentioned in the extended works along with what is against them; refer to them. And we mention them: know that those who assert identity [between command and prohibition of the opposite], al-Qadi among them said: First, if commanding something is not prohibiting its opposite, then they are either similar or opposite or different. On the first two, it would be impossible for them to coexist, but they do coexist necessarily since there is no impossibility in commanding something and prohibiting its opposite, and vice versa. On the third, it is possible for a command of something to coexist with the opposite of prohibition of the opposite, and its opposite is commanded by it, so it would necessitate the combination of command of something and command of its opposite; this is a fallacy. We say they are different, and we do not concede the necessity of the possibility of command of something with the opposite of prohibition of the opposite, for it is permissible for there to be a necessary connection between command and prohibition of the opposite, so separation is not valid. Rather, implication necessarily occurs as you have learned; perhaps for this reason al-Qadi retracted from identity to implication. Secondly, that stillness is the abandonment of motion, so commanding stillness is a request for abandonment of motion, which is prohibition of motion. We say: the opposites that are negations of commanded acts are conceded to be identical to their abandonment, but they are outside the dispute, for it is about particular established opposites. As for the claim that every opposite is commanded as its abandonment, it is prevented; for eating is not itself the abandonment of prayer. Rather, abandonment of the opposite is among the necessary concomitants of the existence of the commanded act, so the command is a necessary condition for the prohibition of the opposite. The specializer thought identity or implication in the command, but prohibition only requires negation of the act, and the existence of the opposite is not identical to it nor a necessary concomitant due to the possibility of the act's negation by the absence of the motive, not due to the presence of an obstacle. And it has already been mentioned that it is valid. As for the necessity of obligatory unlawful acts, the answer has already been given. As for the necessity of negation of permissible acts, its case will come later, God willing. So the specializer thought regarding the command of obligation one of the last two, for there is no time except that there is a recommended act in it, so every permissible would become disliked. Its case will be clarified later, God willing. Those who deny identity and implication said: If commanding something is itself the prohibition of the opposite or a necessary condition for it, and vice versa, from command of something and prohibition of something, opposites are conceived, and the consequent is necessarily false. As for the necessary connection, because it necessitates: no command or prohibition is conceived without conceiving their objects. We say: the necessity of conception is in cases where the obligation is by an explicit command or prohibition or by an obvious necessary concomitant of an explicit obligation; the matter here is not like that, for the prohibition of the opposite is a necessary concomitant of the command in a non-obvious way, even if it is claimed to be obvious in a general sense. And it is answered in the well-known view that what is negated is the conception of a specific opposite; as for the conception of absolute opposition, it is necessary, because command only occurs at the time of the absence of the commanded act, otherwise it would be a request for the already existent, and its absence only occurs by engagement in the opposite, so conception of absolute opposition becomes necessary. The conclusion is that conception of particular opposites in detail is not necessary, and its absence is conceded. As for their conception in a general sense as opposition, it is necessarily required and realized here, so it is not rebutted that if the negation of conception of a specific opposite is conceded, then what the proponent claimed is conceded, for the discussion is about whether particular opposites are prohibited or not, as in al-Tahrir. Nor is it rebutted that he first affirmed the negation of conception of particular opposites and then later affirmed conception of some opposite, as also in al-Tahrir. An objection to this answer was raised: first, that the absence of the commanded act at the time of command is not necessary; rather, all that is necessary is the absence of the commanded act in the future, so conception of the opposite is not necessary. This is insufficient, for the respondent can say that conception of the absence of the commanded act in the future and engagement in its opposite is necessary, and this suffices. A more appropriate objection is that command does not require conception of absence even in the future; do you not see that the obedient one is commanded by God and His knowledge encompasses everything, and likewise absence at the time of command is not necessary, for the believer is commanded with faith in the future? Rather, it is necessary to conceive that it is not attained without the action of the commanded one, and its absence can occur by the will of God, and that does not entail conception of the opposite at all. Second, that all that is necessitated is conception of the opposite, but the proponent did not negate it; rather, he negated conception of the opposite as prohibited or demanded. For his purpose: if the command were itself the prohibition of the opposite or its necessary condition, and vice versa, then conception of opposites would be necessitated in the command as prohibited and in the prohibition as commanded, for command and prohibition of something are not conceived without conceiving it in this manner of conception. The correct answer is what we have mentioned. There has been some verbosity here, and after this, hidden matters remain, and upon Him is reliance.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage is a detailed usul dispute about the relationship between a command (amr) and the prohibition of its opposite (nahy 'an al-didd). The discussion arises from the question: Does commanding an act necessarily imply prohibiting its opposite? Two extreme positions are presented: 'identity' (al-'ayniyya) and 'implication' (al-tadammun). The text first presents the arguments of al-Qadi, who initially held identity but later retracted to implication. The Hanafi position (implied by the author) is that the command does not entail prohibition of the opposite at all; rather, the opposite is not prohibited by the command alone. The objection concerning the necessity of conceiving opposites is addressed: the command only requires a general conception of opposition, not a detailed conception of specific opposites. The Hanafi response shows that a command for a future act does not require the commanded person to conceive of the specific opposite at the time of command. This is a classic usul dispute that distinguishes the Hanafi school from the Mutakallimun regarding the entailments of commands.

Key Terms
العينية (al-'ayniyya): The claim that the command to do something is identical to the prohibition of its opposite.
التضمن (al-tadammun): The claim that the command to do something implies (entails) the prohibition of its opposite, without being identical.
الضد (al-didd): An opposite act that precludes the commanded act.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position presented in this passage, what is the relationship between a command to do something and the prohibition of its opposite?
A) The command is identical to the prohibition of its opposite.
B) The command implies the prohibition of its opposite.
C) The command does not entail the prohibition of its opposite.
D) The command and prohibition of the opposite are mutually exclusive.

Key Takeaway
A command (amr) does not entail the prohibition of its opposite; each is an independent legal category requiring its own evidence.

Mantuq and Mafhum

8 min
The Hanafi Critique of the Condition for Metaphor: Mentioning the Compared Thing
Linguistic critique of a condition for metaphor

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the Hanafi argument that metaphor (isti'ara) does not require omitting the subject of comparison (al-mushabbah), using evidence from the Qur'an and poetry.

Core Idea
Metaphor is valid even if the compared thing is mentioned; the contrary claim lacks proof.

This lesson explores the linguistic foundation of the dispute over 'أنت ابني'. The author first refutes the claim by scholars of rhetoric that metaphor must omit the subject. By citing Qur'anic examples, he shows that the condition is not universally accepted, paving the way for the legal analysis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Contested Condition
Recognize that scholars of rhetoric (al-bayān) claim that metaphor (isti‘ārah) requires that the subject of comparison (al-mushabbah) not be mentioned. If mentioned, the phrase becomes a simile (tashbīh).
2
Understand the Author’s Rebuttal
The author argues that this rule is not supported by evidence. He gives examples from the Qur’an (e.g., 'the white thread from the black thread of dawn') and poetry where the subject is mentioned yet the phrase is clearly metaphorical. This refutes the universal condition.
Focus on the verse from Sūrat al-Baqarah and the example 'išt‘ala al-ra’su šayban'.
3
Consider the Alternative: Eloquent Simile
Even if we accept that 'أنت ابني' is a simile, the author argues it is an eloquent simile (tashbīh balīgh) that implies identity, thus still effecting freedom. This shows that the outcome does not depend solely on the linguistic category.
Eloquent simile claims the object is the very thing compared, not just similar.
4
Evaluate the Hanafi Position
The author concludes that the condition is invalid, so the phrase can be treated as a metaphor. This linguistic flexibility supports the later legal classification as an acknowledgment or creation depending on context.
The lesson here is that Hanafi usul does not rigidly adhere to rhetoric rules when they conflict with legal objectives.

And making explicit words a complete cause, whether literal or figurative, and 'you are my son' is explicit, as stated by Sadr al-Shariah and others. For the impossibility of the literal turns the word to the figurative clearly, no hiddenness in it. Similarly its becoming customary. So in it that cutting is not a cause for wealth absolutely, rather if he cuts mistakenly, wealth is obligated in a particular manner. So the relation is insufficient for transition customarily, not due to the lack of correctness of the literal ruling. And the summary of the answer is that correctness of construction does not necessitate correctness of metaphor, for the occurrence of one condition does not necessitate the occurrence of the conditioned due to the possibility of absence of another condition. Perhaps this is the absence of the corrective relation, for cutting is not a cause for absolute wealth, but for particular wealth whose obligation is not valid, and the mind does not transfer from cutting to absolute wealth at all. Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage from Fawatih al-Rahmut, a commentary on Musallam al-Thubut, addresses a key usul issue: the legal nature of the phrase 'أنت ابني' (you are my son) when spoken to a slave. The discussion is driven by a dispute over whether this phrase constitutes an acknowledgment (إقرار) of prior freedom or a creation (إنشاء) of new freedom. The text first engages in a linguistic analysis to determine if the phrase is a metaphor (استعارة) or a simile (تشبيه). The author argues against the claim that metaphor requires not mentioning the subject being compared (المشبه), citing Quranic verses like 'the white thread from the black thread of dawn' where dawn is mentioned yet the phrase is metaphorical. The author then shifts to the legal ruling: some say it is an acknowledgment—so the slave is freed only judicially (قضاء) and the mother becomes umm walad only if the owner truly intended manumission; others say it is a creation—so both judicial and religious freedom, and the mother is not umm walad. The author prefers the first view, supported by Imam Muhammad's statement in al-Mabsut: coercion prevents acknowledgment but not creation, and since coercion voids the phrase's effect, it must be an acknowledgment. The author critiques this reasoning, arguing that the figurative meaning does not necessarily require intention; the phrase is explicit (صريح) due to the impossibility of the literal meaning. Therefore, if it were creation, it would be valid under coercion. Its invalidity shows it is acknowledgment. The text then addresses a counterargument about priority: whether the subordination (الخلفية) should be to the ruling or to linguistic correctness. The author argues for preserving linguistic correctness (صون اللفظ عن اللغو) as primary, because speech aims at communication. The example of 'قطعت يدك' (I cut your hand) is given to show that not every syntactically correct phrase allows metaphor; there must be an appropriate relation (علاقة) to justify the transition. This detailed analysis demonstrates how usul principles (literal vs. figurative, linguistic vs. legal priority) directly impact a concrete fiqh ruling on manumission and the status of the slave's mother.

Key Terms
استعارة (isti'ārah): Metaphor: using a word in a meaning other than its original by way of resemblance, where the subject of comparison is not explicitly mentioned.
تشبيه (tashbīh): Simile: explicit comparison using an instrument (like 'like') or implying equality in the aspect of resemblance.
مجاز (majāz): Figurative speech: using a word in a non-literal meaning due to a relationship between them.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the main linguistic argument the author uses to counter the claim that metaphor requires omitting the subject?
A) He cites Imam Muhammad's statement from al-Mabsut.
B) He provides Qur'anic examples where the subject is mentioned yet the phrase is metaphorical.
C) He argues that 'أنت ابني' is not a metaphor at all.
D) He relies on consensus of later scholars.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school rejects the claim that metaphor must omit the subject; 'أنت ابني' can be interpreted metaphorically, supporting its legal classification as an acknowledgment.

Mantuq and Mafhum
advanced
8 min
The Hanafi Ruling: 'أنت ابني' as Acknowledgment (Iqrar) of Freedom
Legal classification of a phrase as iqrar vs insha'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi classification of 'أنت ابني' as an acknowledgment (iqrar) of freedom by analysing the proof from coercion and the resulting thamarah al-khilaf.

Core Idea
Coherence: the phrase's invalidity under coercion proves it is acknowledgment, not creation.

After the linguistic analysis, the passage directly addresses the legal nature of 'أنت ابني'. Two positions emerge: creation (insha') or acknowledgment (iqrar). The author favours the latter, using a crucial proof from Imam Muhammad's al-Mabsut: coercion negates acknowledgment but not creation. Since coercion voids the effect, the phrase must be acknowledgment.

Guided Path
1
State the Two Conflicting Views
Identify the two positions: 1) the phrase is an acknowledgment (iqrar) of prior freedom – only judicial freedom, mother becomes umm walad only if intention; 2) it is a creation (insha') of freedom – both judicial and religious freedom, mother does not become umm walad.
2
Understand the Proof from Coercion
Imam Muhammad's statement in al-Mabsut: coercion prevents the validity of acknowledgment but not creation. Apply this: if the phrase were creation, it would be valid under coercion; but it is not, so it must be acknowledgment.
The exact Arabic quote: 'إذا أكره على قول هذا ابني لعبده لا يعتق عليه والإكراه إنما يمنع صحة الإقرار بالعتق لا إنشاءه'.
3
Analyse the Counter-Argument
Some object that the figurative meaning may not require intention; the phrase could be explicit (sarih) and thus creation. The author responds: if it were explicit creation, it would be valid under coercion, but it is not, confirming it is acknowledgment.
This counter-argument is addressed in the passage, and the author refutes it.
4
Determine the Thamarah al-Khilaf
Practical consequences: under acknowledgment, the slave is freed only judicially (qada'), and the mother becomes umm walad only if the owner intended the full adoption rulings. Under creation, the slave is freed both judicially and religiously, and the mother does not become umm walad.
This difference is crucial for fatwa.

Not mentioning it as forgotten? It is like: Zayd is a lion, and it is a simile as stated by the scholars of rhetoric. There is no manumission in simile, so he is not freed in this like the free man? It is invalid. Not because of what is in al-Talwih that the compared thing is the absolute free man, and what is mentioned here is specific, because this type of mentioning is also not permitted by the scholars of rhetoric, until they ruled that 'Zayd is a lion' is a simile, so that the author of al-Kashshaf interpreted the verse {Deaf, dumb, blind} as simile, and they said mentioning the compared thing in speech such that it indicates simile is prohibited in metaphor. Even more far-fetched is what is in al-Tawdih that what is prohibited is only when the compared thing is the subject and the predicate is a noun, and here 'the son' is derived so metaphor is valid, as in 'the state is speaking' and its flaw is not hidden. Rather because this statement is what the scholars of rhetoric claimed and did not establish with proof at all, so their statement is not considered, but the apparent from usage is contrary, as in His statement {Eat and drink until the white thread becomes clear to you from the black thread of dawn} for the compared thing which is dawn is mentioned in a way that indicates simile, although what is intended by the white thread is dawn metaphorically, otherwise the clarification by it would not be valid. Similarly in His statement {And the head became aflame with gray hair} for gray hair is intended by the head's aflame, otherwise it would not occur as a discriminator. And in the poet's saying: 'A lion against me and in wars an ostrich? hears' so what is intended by lion is the bold one, otherwise the adverb would not be attached to it. And many examples like this. In summary, the condition in metaphor for not mentioning the compared thing is not supported by induction nor any evidence at all, so their statement is rejected. This, but you may say: we accept that metaphor is conditioned by that, and that 'Zayd is a lion' is a simile, but this simile is not that the tool is implied? How? Then it would be non-eloquent speech and would not be an eloquent simile. Rather the meaning is that the speaker intended complete simile and strength of similarity in the aspect of resemblance, so it is claimed that Zayd is the very lion by way of figurative attribution. So 'this is my son' if it is an eloquent simile, its meaning is that his similarity to the son in attachment from the time of ownership reached that he became the very son. In this, manumission is necessary absolutely. And it is not like 'like my son' because he did not claim in it that he is an individual of the son such that manumission is necessary. So understand, for it is a useful opportunity for us that the transition to figurative from the literal meaning, for it is understood from the word and the evidence indicates that it is not intended, so he transitions to the figurative. (And it) i.e., the transition from the literal meaning (depends on the correctness of speech from the perspective of Arabic, for by correctness of Arabic what is put for it in that language is understood, then from it he transitions to its associations (not) correctness (of the ruling). Do you not see that from the word at absolute impossibility it is understood, as is not hidden from a clever person. So then there is no subordination except from the perspective of the speaker, and this is more appropriate than what is famous that metaphor is a change in the word from a state to a state, so the subordination is from the perspective of the word, not from the perspective of the ruling. For it is objected to him that it is accepted that the change is in the word, but it does not necessitate that the subordination is from the perspective of the speaker; rather the most that is necessitated is that the word insofar as it is changed is subordinate to itself insofar as it is changed from it. As for the aspect of substitutability, it is not known what it is and has not been determined yet. And it is possible to answer that since figurative usage is a change of the word from one meaning to another, in this change nothing is expected except correctness of communication, and that is by correctness of construction according to Arabic grammar, and it does not depend on the correctness of the ruling itself, for it has no role in communication. So reflect. (Then it was said) 'You are my son' (is an acknowledgment) of freedom from the time of ownership, so on this view he is freed judicially. As for religiously, if he has truly manumitted, then he is freed, otherwise not, and his mother becomes an umm walad for him. I say: there is something in it. Because although it is an acknowledgment, it is an acknowledgment of freedom, not of sonship. What necessitates the mother being umm walad is the second, not the first. Unless it is said that he intended by this expression adoption and establishment of all rulings of sons from freedom from the time of ownership, mother being umm walad, and inheritance, until it became customary in it, but when the Law abrogated adoption and inheritance, freedom remained in it, and in his mother the right of freedom as it was. So reflect on it. (And it was said) it is not an acknowledgment (but a creation) of manumission like 'you are free from the time of ownership'. On this view, he is freed both judicially and religiously, and his mother does not become umm walad. (In al-Tahrir the first) i.e., it being an acknowledgment is more correct, because of the statement of Imam Muhammad in the book of al-Ikrah from al-Mabsut: if a man is coerced into saying 'this is my son' to his slave, it does not effect freedom for him, and coercion only (prevents the validity of acknowledgment of freedom, not its creation) so it is known that it is an acknowledgment. (I say) the non-freedom in it is not due to the invalidity of the acknowledgment such that if it were creation it would free; rather because the figurative depends on intention, for the word is for the literal, so it is precedent except by intention to turn away from it, and coercion is a place of weakness of will and intention, so nothing is established there except what makes the word alone a complete cause for it, not what is established by intention. So this, due to its dependence on intention, is not valid as acknowledgment or creation under coercion. And this statement is not well-directed, for the dependence of a figurative meaning on intention is not necessary. Rather what depends on intention is metonymy, whether literal or figurative. And making explicit words a complete cause, whether literal or figurative, and 'you are my son' is explicit, as stated by Sadr al-Shariah and others. For the impossibility of the literal turns the word to the figurative clearly, no hiddenness in it. Similarly its becoming customary. So if it were creation, it would not depend on intention and would be valid under coercion. So its invalidity under coercion indicates that it is an acknowledgment. So reflect. (And for them both that the ruling is what is intended from the word, so substitutability by its consideration is more appropriate. And the answer is that this is prevented. How? And there is no suitability for intentionality with regard to meaning, rather substitutability here is in the construction according to the grammar of the language. (I say) we do not accept that substitutability with respect to the ruling is more appropriate. Rather preservation, i.e., preservation of the word, from nonsense which would be necessitated by substitutability in the ruling, is more appropriate, because speech is for communication, not for nullification. So reflect. For them both may say: yes, preservation is more appropriate, but wherever possible, and here it is not possible due to the absence of the condition of metaphor. But the matter is not hidden from the reflective person. (And as for their statement in the proof: 'I cut your hand' if he brings them out sound and does not make this speech (figurative) as acknowledgment of wealth, and the construction is sound, and cutting is a cause for the obligation of wealth, so it is known that possibility of the literal is a condition and it is absent. So in it that cutting is not a cause for wealth absolutely, rather if he cuts mistakenly, wealth is obligated in a particular manner. So the relation is insufficient for transition customarily, not due to the lack of correctness of the literal ruling. And the summary of the answer is that correctness of construction does not necessitate correctness of metaphor, for the occurrence of one condition does not necessitate the occurrence of the conditioned due to the possibility of absence of another condition. Perhaps this is the absence of the corrective relation, for cutting is not a cause for absolute wealth, but for particular wealth whose obligation is not valid, and the mind does not transfer from cutting to absolute wealth at all. So the indication, and it is dependent on correctness...
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage from Fawatih al-Rahmut, a commentary on Musallam al-Thubut, addresses a key usul issue: the legal nature of the phrase 'أنت ابني' (you are my son) when spoken to a slave. The discussion is driven by a dispute over whether this phrase constitutes an acknowledgment (إقرار) of prior freedom or a creation (إنشاء) of new freedom. The text first engages in a linguistic analysis to determine if the phrase is a metaphor (استعارة) or a simile (تشبيه). The author argues against the claim that metaphor requires not mentioning the subject being compared (المشبه), citing Quranic verses like 'the white thread from the black thread of dawn' where dawn is mentioned yet the phrase is metaphorical. The author then shifts to the legal ruling: some say it is an acknowledgment—so the slave is freed only judicially (قضاء) and the mother becomes umm walad only if the owner truly intended manumission; others say it is a creation—so both judicial and religious freedom, and the mother is not umm walad. The author prefers the first view, supported by Imam Muhammad's statement in al-Mabsut: coercion prevents acknowledgment but not creation, and since coercion voids the phrase's effect, it must be an acknowledgment. The author critiques this reasoning, arguing that the figurative meaning does not necessarily require intention; the phrase is explicit (صريح) due to the impossibility of the literal meaning. Therefore, if it were creation, it would be valid under coercion. Its invalidity shows it is acknowledgment. The text then addresses a counterargument about priority: whether the subordination (الخلفية) should be to the ruling or to linguistic correctness. The author argues for preserving linguistic correctness (صون اللفظ عن اللغو) as primary, because speech aims at communication. The example of 'قطعت يدك' (I cut your hand) is given to show that not every syntactically correct phrase allows metaphor; there must be an appropriate relation (علاقة) to justify the transition. This detailed analysis demonstrates how usul principles (literal vs. figurative, linguistic vs. legal priority) directly impact a concrete fiqh ruling on manumission and the status of the slave's mother.

Key Terms
إقرار (iqrār): Acknowledgment: admitting a pre-existing fact (here, freedom from the time of ownership).
إنشاء (inshā'): Creation: bringing about a new legal effect (here, freedom) by the utterance itself.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the author prefer the view that 'أنت ابني' is an acknowledgment (iqrar) rather than a creation (insha')?
A) Because the phrase is not explicit enough to create freedom.
B) Because if it were creation, it would be valid under coercion, but it is not.
C) Because the owner must intend to free the slave.
D) Because the mother cannot be umm walad.

Key Takeaway
'أنت ابني' to a slave is an acknowledgment (iqrar) of freedom, not a creation, as proven by its invalidity under coercion.

Mantuq and Mafhum
advanced
8 min
Priority in Usul: Subordination to Linguistic Correctness or to the Ruling?
Usul discussion on priority in figurative interpretation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the Hanafi position that in figurative interpretation, preservation of linguistic correctness takes precedence over the desired ruling, using the example of 'قطعت يدك'.

Core Idea
Linguistic correctness (صون اللفظ عن اللغو) is prioritized over the ruling in determining figurative meaning.

The passage culminates in a broader usul debate: when a word can be both literal and figurative, should we prioritize the ruling (hukm) or the linguistic structure? The author argues for linguistic correctness, because speech aims at communication, not at legal effects. This is demonstrated with the example of 'I cut your hand' not being automatically figurative for compensation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Two Rival Criteria
Some (them) argue that the ruling is the goal of speech, so subordination (khilafiyya) should be to the ruling. The author argues that preservation of the word from nonsense (صون اللفظ عن اللغو) is primary.
2
Understand the Author's Argument
The author asserts that if we prioritize the ruling, we might interpret speech in a way that makes it meaningless. Correctness of speech (Arabic syntax) is the foundation for understanding, and only then can we consider the ruling.
Quote: 'الكلام للإفادة لا للإلغاء'.
3
Analyse the Example of 'قطعت يدك'
The opponents give an example to support their view: 'I cut your hand' if both hands are sound, it cannot be literal, so it must be figurative for acknowledgment of wealth. The author counters that the relation (علاقة) between cutting and wealth is insufficient for the metaphor; not every syntactically correct sentence becomes figurative.
The example shows that even if the literal is impossible, metaphor requires an appropriate relationship.
4
Conclude the Usul Principle
The Hanafi principle is that subordination is based on linguistic correctness, not the ruling. The existence of a syntactic condition does not automatically create a valid metaphor; there must be a customary relation.

الخاص، لأن هذا النحو من الذكر أيضاً لم يجوّزه علماء البيان، حتى حكموا بأن نحو زيد أسد تشبيه، حتى حمل صاحب الكشاف قوله تعالى: {صم بكم عمى} على التشبيه وقالوا ذكر المشبه في الكلام بحيث ينبيء عن التشبيه ممنوع في الاستعارة، وأبعد من هذا ما في التوضيح أن الممنوع إنما هو إذا كان المشبه مبتدأ والخبر جامداً، وههنا الابن مشتق فيصح الاستعارة، كما في الحال ناطقة ولا يخفى ما فيه، بل لأن هذا القول مما ادعته علماء البيان ولم يصححوه ببرهان أصلاً، فلا اعتبار لقولهم بل الظاهر من الاستعمال خلافه، كما في قوله تعالى: {وكلوا واشربوا حتى يتبين لكم الخيط الأبيض من الخيط الأسود من الفجر} فإن المشبه الذي هو الفجر مذكور على نحو ينبيء عن التشبيه، مع أنه أريد من الخيط الأبيض الفجر مجازاً، وإلا لم يصح البيان به، وكذا في قوله تعالى: {واشتعل الرأس شيباً} فإن الشيب هو المراد باشتعال الرأس وإلا لم يقع مميزاً، وفي قول الشاعر: أسد علي وفي الحروب نعامة يسمع فأريد من الأسد المجترىء وإلا لما تعلق الظرف به وأمثال هذا كثيرة، وبالجملة صح الاشتراط في الاستعارة لعدم ذكر المشبه مما لم يؤيده استقراء ولا شاهد عليه أصلاً فلا قولهم، هذا ولك أن تقول: سلمنا أن الاستعارة مشروطة بذلك، وأن نحو زيد أسد تشبيه، فليس هذا التشبيه بأن تكون الأداة مقدرة كيف وحينئذ يكون كلاماً غير فصيح ولم يكن تشبيهاً بليغاً، بل المعنى أن المتكلم قصد منه التشبيه الكامل وقوة المشابهة في وجه الشبه، فادعي أن زيداً عين الأسد على طريق الإسناد المجازي، فهذا ابني إن كان تشبيهاً بليغاً، فيكون معناه أن مشابهته للابن في التعلق من حين الملك بلغت إلى أن صار عين الابن، وفي هذا الاعتاق لازم قطعاً، وليس مثل هذا مثل ابني فإنه لم يدع فيه كونه من أفراد الابن حتى يلزم العتق فافهم فإنه سانح عزيز لنا أن الانتقال إلى المجازي من المعنى الحقيقي فإنه فهم من اللفظ ودل القرينة على أنه غير مراد انتقل إلى المجازي (وهو) أي الانتقال من المعنى الحقيقي (يعتمد صحة الكلام من حيث العربية، إذ بصحة العربية يفهم ما وضع له في تلك اللغة، فينتقل منه إلى ملابساته (لا) صحة (الحكم ألا ترى أنه يفهم من اللفظ عند الإطلاق المحال كما لا يخفى على ذي كياسة فإذن لا فرعية إلا من جهة التكلم وهذا أولى مما هو المشهور أن المجاز تغيير في اللفظ من حال إلى حال فتكون الفرعية من جهة اللفظ، لا من جهة الحكم فإنه يرد عليه أنه مسلم أن التغيير فيه في اللفظ، لكن لا يلزم أن التغيير فيه في اللفظ، لكن لا يلزم منه أن الفرعية من جهة التكلم بل غاية ما لزم أن اللفظ من حيث إنه متغير فرع لنفسه من حيث إنه متغير عنه، وأما جهة الخلفية فلم يعلم أنه أي شيء ولم يتعين بعد، ويمكن أن يجاب عنه بأن التجوز لما كان تغيير اللفظ من معنى إلى آخر فلا ينتظر في هذا التغيير إلا إلى صحة الإفادة، وذا بصحة التركيب على القانون العربي، وهو لا يتوقف على صحة الحكم في نفسه فإنه مما لا دخل له في الإفادة فتدبر (ثم قيل) أنت ابني (إقرار) للحرية من وقت الملك فعلى هذا يعتق قضاء وأما ديانة فإن كان تحقق منه الإعتاق فيعتق وإلا لا فتصير أمه أم ولد له، أقول: وفيه ما فيه) لأنه وإن كان إقراراً، لكنه إقرار بالحرية لا بالبنوة، والمستلزم لأمومية الأم هو الثاني لا الأول، إلاّ أن يقال إنه كان يقصد من هذه العبارة التبني وثبوت جميع أحكام الأبناء من العتق من حين الملك وأمومية الأم والميراث حتى صار عرفاً فيه إلا أن الشرع لما نسخ التبني والميراث بقي العتق فيه، وفي أمه حق العتق كما كان فتأمل فيه (وقيل) ليس بإقرار (بل إنشاء) للإعتاق بمنزلة أنت حرّ من حين الملك، وعلى هذا يعتق قضاء وديانة فلا تصير أمه أم ولد له (وفي التحرير الأول) أي كونه إقراراً أصح لقوله أي قول الإمام محمد (في) كتاب (الإكراه) من المبسوط إذا أكره) رجل (على) قول هذا ابني لعبده لا يعتق عليه والإكراه إنما (يمنع صحة الإقرار بالعتق لا انشاءه) فعلم أنه إقرار (أقول) ليس عدم العتق فيه لعدم صحة الإقرار حتى لو كان إنشاء يعتق بل لأن المجازي يتوقف على النية لأن اللفظ للحقيقة فهي الأسبق إلا بنية انصرافه عنها والإكراه محل فتور الإرادة والقصد فلا يثبت هناك إلا ما جعل اللفظ فقط علة تامة له) لا ما يثبت بالنية، فهذا لتوقفه على النية لا يصح إقراراً كان أو إنشاء حال الإكراه، وهذا الكلام غير موجه فإنه ليس يلزم توقف على معنى مجازي على النية، بل المتوقف عليها هي الكناية، سواء كانت حقيقة أو مجازاً، وجعل الألفاظ الصريحة علة تامة، سواء كانت حقائق أو مجازات، وأنت ابني من الصريح صرح به صدر الشريعة وغيره فإن استحالة الحقيقي صرف اللفظ إلى المجازي صرفاً ظاهراً لا خفاء فيه، وكذا صيرورته عرفاً، فلو كان إنشاء لم يتوقف على النية وصح في الإكراه، فعدم صحته حال الإكراه دل على كونه إقراراً فتدبر (ولهما أن الحكم هو المقصود من اللفظ (فالخلفية باعتباره أولى والجواب أن هذا ممنوع، كيف ولا ملاءمة للمقصودية باعتبارها فيما يتعلق بالدلالة، بل الخلفية ههنا في التركيب على قانون اللغة (أقول) لا نسلم أن الخلفية بالنظر إلى الحكم أولى بل الصون) أي صون اللفظ (عن (اللغو) الذي يلزم على الخلفية في الحكم (أولى) لأن الكلام للإفادة لا للإلغاء فتأمل، فإن لهما أن يقولا نعم الصون أولى لكن مهما أمكن وههنا غير ممكن لانتفاء شرط المجاز، لكن الأمر غير خفي على المتأمل (وأما قولهما في الاستدلال (لغي قطعت يدك إذا أخرجهما صحيحين ولم يجعل هذا الكلام (مجازاً) عن الإقرار بالمال) والتركيب صحيح والقطع سبب لوجوب المال، فعلم أن إمكان الحقيقي شرط وقد انتفى ففيه أن القطع ليس سبباً للمال مطلقاً بل إذا قطع خطأ يجب المال على وجه مخصوص فالعلاقة قاصرة لا تكفي للانتقال عرفاً لا لعدم صحة حكم الحقيقة، وحاصل الجواب أنه لا يلزم من صحة التركيب صحة المجاز، إذ لا يلزم من تحقق شرط واحد تحقق المشروط لاحتمال فقدان شرط آخر، ولعل هذا عدم تحقق العلاقة المصححة فإنه ليس القطع سبباً للمال المطلق: بل للمال المخصوص الذي لا يصح وجوبه، ولا ينتقل الذهن من القطع إلى المال المطلق أصلاً، فلا الدلالة وهي تابعية لصحة
Not mentioning it as forgotten? It is like: Zayd is a lion, and it is a simile as stated by the scholars of rhetoric. There is no manumission in simile, so he is not freed in this like the free man? It is invalid. Not because of what is in al-Talwih that the compared thing is the absolute free man, and what is mentioned here is specific, because this type of mentioning is also not permitted by the scholars of rhetoric, until they ruled that 'Zayd is a lion' is a simile, so that the author of al-Kashshaf interpreted the verse {Deaf, dumb, blind} as simile, and they said mentioning the compared thing in speech such that it indicates simile is prohibited in metaphor. Even more far-fetched is what is in al-Tawdih that what is prohibited is only when the compared thing is the subject and the predicate is a noun, and here 'the son' is derived so metaphor is valid, as in 'the state is speaking' and its flaw is not hidden. Rather because this statement is what the scholars of rhetoric claimed and did not establish with proof at all, so their statement is not considered, but the apparent from usage is contrary, as in His statement {Eat and drink until the white thread becomes clear to you from the black thread of dawn} for the compared thing which is dawn is mentioned in a way that indicates simile, although what is intended by the white thread is dawn metaphorically, otherwise the clarification by it would not be valid. Similarly in His statement {And the head became aflame with gray hair} for gray hair is intended by the head's aflame, otherwise it would not occur as a discriminator. And in the poet's saying: 'A lion against me and in wars an ostrich? hears' so what is intended by lion is the bold one, otherwise the adverb would not be attached to it. And many examples like this. In summary, the condition in metaphor for not mentioning the compared thing is not supported by induction nor any evidence at all, so their statement is rejected. This, but you may say: we accept that metaphor is conditioned by that, and that 'Zayd is a lion' is a simile, but this simile is not that the tool is implied? How? Then it would be non-eloquent speech and would not be an eloquent simile. Rather the meaning is that the speaker intended complete simile and strength of similarity in the aspect of resemblance, so it is claimed that Zayd is the very lion by way of figurative attribution. So 'this is my son' if it is an eloquent simile, its meaning is that his similarity to the son in attachment from the time of ownership reached that he became the very son. In this, manumission is necessary absolutely. And it is not like 'like my son' because he did not claim in it that he is an individual of the son such that manumission is necessary. So understand, for it is a useful opportunity for us that the transition to figurative from the literal meaning, for it is understood from the word and the evidence indicates that it is not intended, so he transitions to the figurative. Do you not see that from the word at absolute impossibility it is understood, as is not hidden from a clever person. So then there is no subordination except from the perspective of the speaker, and this is more appropriate than what is famous that metaphor is a change in the word from a state to a state, so the subordination is from the perspective of the word, not from the perspective of the ruling. For it is objected to him that it is accepted that the change is in the word, but it does not necessitate that the subordination is from the perspective of the speaker; rather the most that is necessitated is that the word insofar as it is changed is subordinate to itself insofar as it is changed from it. As for the aspect of substitutability, it is not known what it is and has not been determined yet. And it is possible to answer that since figurative usage is a change of the word from one meaning to another, in this change nothing is expected except correctness of communication, and that is by correctness of construction according to Arabic grammar, and it does not depend on the correctness of the ruling itself, for it has no role in communication. So reflect. (Then it was said) 'You are my son' (is an acknowledgment) of freedom from the time of ownership, so on this view he is freed judicially. As for religiously, if he has truly manumitted, then he is freed, otherwise not, and his mother becomes an umm walad for him. I say: there is something in it. Because although it is an acknowledgment, it is an acknowledgment of freedom, not of sonship. What necessitates the mother being umm walad is the second, not the first. Unless it is said that he intended by this expression adoption and establishment of all rulings of sons from freedom from the time of ownership, mother being umm walad, and inheritance, until it became customary in it, but when the Law abrogated adoption and inheritance, freedom remained in it, and in his mother the right of freedom as it was. So reflect on it. (And it was said) it is not an acknowledgment (but a creation) of manumission like 'you are free from the time of ownership'. On this view, he is freed both judicially and religiously, and his mother does not become umm walad. (In al-Tahrir the first) i.e., it being an acknowledgment is more correct, because of the statement of Imam Muhammad in the book of al-Ikrah from al-Mabsut: if a man is coerced into saying 'this is my son' to his slave, it does not effect freedom for him, and coercion only (prevents the validity of acknowledgment of freedom, not its creation) so it is known that it is an acknowledgment. (I say) the non-freedom in it is not due to the invalidity of the acknowledgment such that if it were creation it would free; rather because the figurative depends on intention, for the word is for the literal, so it is precedent except by intention to turn away from it, and coercion is a place of weakness of will and intention, so nothing is established there except what makes the word alone a complete cause for it, not what is established by intention. So this, due to its dependence on intention, is not valid as acknowledgment or creation under coercion. And this statement is not well-directed, for the dependence of a figurative meaning on intention is not necessary. Rather what depends on intention is metonymy, whether literal or figurative. And making explicit words a complete cause, whether literal or figurative, and 'you are my son' is explicit, as stated by Sadr al-Shariah and others. For the impossibility of the literal turns the word to the figurative clearly, no hiddenness in it. Similarly its becoming customary. So if it were creation, it would not depend on intention and would be valid under coercion. So its invalidity under coercion indicates that it is an acknowledgment. So reflect. (And for them both that the ruling is what is intended from the word, so substitutability by its consideration is more appropriate. And the answer is that this is prevented. How? And there is no suitability for intentionality with regard to meaning, rather substitutability here is in the construction according to the grammar of the language. (I say) we do not accept that substitutability with respect to the ruling is more appropriate. Rather preservation, i.e., preservation of the word, from nonsense which would be necessitated by substitutability in the ruling, is more appropriate, because speech is for communication, not for nullification. So reflect. For them both may say: yes, preservation is more appropriate, but wherever possible, and here it is not possible due to the absence of the condition of metaphor. But the matter is not hidden from the reflective person. So in it that cutting is not a cause for wealth absolutely, rather if he cuts mistakenly, wealth is obligated in a particular manner. So the relation is insufficient for transition customarily, not due to the lack of correctness of the literal ruling. And the summary of the answer is that correctness of construction does not necessitate correctness of metaphor, for the occurrence of one condition does not necessitate the occurrence of the conditioned due to the possibility of absence of another condition. Perhaps this is the absence of the corrective relation, for cutting is not a cause for absolute wealth, but for particular wealth whose obligation is not valid, and the mind does not transfer from cutting to absolute wealth at all. So the indication, and it is dependent on correctness...
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage from Fawatih al-Rahmut, a commentary on Musallam al-Thubut, addresses a key usul issue: the legal nature of the phrase 'أنت ابني' (you are my son) when spoken to a slave. The discussion is driven by a dispute over whether this phrase constitutes an acknowledgment (إقرار) of prior freedom or a creation (إنشاء) of new freedom. The text first engages in a linguistic analysis to determine if the phrase is a metaphor (استعارة) or a simile (تشبيه). The author argues against the claim that metaphor requires not mentioning the subject being compared (المشبه), citing Quranic verses like 'the white thread from the black thread of dawn' where dawn is mentioned yet the phrase is metaphorical. The author then shifts to the legal ruling: some say it is an acknowledgment—so the slave is freed only judicially (قضاء) and the mother becomes umm walad only if the owner truly intended manumission; others say it is a creation—so both judicial and religious freedom, and the mother is not umm walad. The author prefers the first view, supported by Imam Muhammad's statement in al-Mabsut: coercion prevents acknowledgment but not creation, and since coercion voids the phrase's effect, it must be an acknowledgment. The author critiques this reasoning, arguing that the figurative meaning does not necessarily require intention; the phrase is explicit (صريح) due to the impossibility of the literal meaning. Therefore, if it were creation, it would be valid under coercion. Its invalidity shows it is acknowledgment. The text then addresses a counterargument about priority: whether the subordination (الخلفية) should be to the ruling or to linguistic correctness. The author argues for preserving linguistic correctness (صون اللفظ عن اللغو) as primary, because speech aims at communication. The example of 'قطعت يدك' (I cut your hand) is given to show that not every syntactically correct phrase allows metaphor; there must be an appropriate relation (علاقة) to justify the transition. This detailed analysis demonstrates how usul principles (literal vs. figurative, linguistic vs. legal priority) directly impact a concrete fiqh ruling on manumission and the status of the slave's mother.

Key Terms
خلفية (khilafiyya): Subordination: the principle that one part of a phrase is dependent on another for its meaning; here, whether the figurative meaning is subordinate to the ruling or to the linguistic structure.
علاقة (ʿalāqah): Relation: the connection between the literal and figurative meanings that legitimizes the metaphor.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that the subordination (khilafiyya) should be based on linguistic correctness rather than on the ruling?
A) Because the ruling is unknown at the moment of speech.
B) Because preserving linguistic integrity prevents nonsense, and speech is for communication.
C) Because the ruling is always secondary to grammar.
D) Because the opponents' argument is based on a flawed example.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, figurative interpretation prioritizes linguistic correctness and requires a valid relationship; the ruling is not the primary criterion.

6 min
Kinayah's Ambiguity and Its Effect on Hudud
Defines kinayah by its inherent insufficiency and applies it to limit hudud punishments

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define kinayah by its inherent ambiguity (qusur zahir wa khafa sarih) and apply the Hanafi principle that kinayah does not establish hudud punishments due to shubha.

Core Idea
Kinayah's doubt blocks hudud: what is not explicit cannot trigger the most severe penalties.

In Hanafi usul, kinayah is defined not just by its indirectness but by its inherent insufficiency (qusur zahir) and clear hiddenness (khafa sarih). This linguistic ambiguity creates a doubt (shubha) about the intended meaning, which in turn activates the famous principle 'adra'u al-hudud bi al-shubuhat' (avert hudud by doubts). This lesson examines how the definition of kinayah directly impacts criminal liability, using the examples of affirming a slanderer and making indirect references.

Guided Path
1
Identify the defining traits of kinayah
Locate the phrases 'qusur zahir' and 'khafa sarih' and understand they refer to the word's inability to clearly convey one meaning.
2
Understand shubhat al-'adam
Recognize that the ambiguity creates a 'semblance of non-existence' of the intended meaning; it is not that the word has no meaning, but that its specific intent is doubtful.
3
Connect to the hadd-averting principle
Recall the hadith 'adra'u al-hudud bi al-shubuhat' and see how kinayah's shubha triggers this rule, preventing hudud from being established.
4
Analyze the case of 'sadaqta'
Deconstruct the two possible interpretations: 'you are generally truthful' vs. 'you are truthful in the slander.' Explain why the doubt averts the hadd.
5
Analyze the case of ta'rid
Understand that indirect reference (ta'rid) is a subset of kinayah and likewise creates doubt, thus not punished.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفائدة الخامسة: في الكناية قصور ظاهر و خفاء صريح كما ينادي عليه حده، ففيه شبهة العدم أي عدم ما قصد به فإنه يجوز إن كان قصد غيره من المعنى، وكذا فيه شبهة احتمال آخر فلا يثبت به ما يندرىء بالشبهة وهي الحدود وإلا لزم ثبوته مع منافيه. فلا يحد مصدق القاذف أي القائل للقاذف صدقت من غير ذلك المفعول فإنه يحتمل إرادة أن ديدنك الصدق، كما يحتمل أنك صادق في القذف. ولا المعرض به أي بالقذف والتعريض هو أن
The fifth benefit: In kinayah there is apparent insufficiency and clear hiddenness, as its definition indicates. So in it there is a semblance of non-existence, i.e., non-existence of what is intended by it, for it is possible that something other than the intended meaning was meant. Similarly, in it there is a semblance of another possibility, so by it nothing that is averted by doubt (shubha) - which are the hudud - is established; otherwise its establishment would be necessary along with its contradiction. Thus, the one who affirms the slanderer (i.e., the one who says to the slanderer 'you have spoken the truth') without that object [being specified] is not flogged, for it is possible that he intends that your habit is truthfulness, just as it is possible that you are truthful in the slander. And neither is the one who makes an indirect reference (ta'rid) to it, i.e., to slander. And ta'rid is that...
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of kinayah in usul al-fiqh by its characteristic of 'apparent insufficiency and clear hiddenness' (qusur zahir wa khafa' sarih). Because kinayah carries an inherent ambiguity—it may be intended for one meaning or another—it creates a 'semblance of non-existence' (shubhat al-'adam) for the intended meaning. This doubt (shubha) is significant in Islamic criminal law: the Prophet ﷺ said, 'Avert hudud by doubts' (adra'u al-hudud bi al-shubuhat). Therefore, kinayah cannot be used to establish a hadd punishment. The text gives two examples: (1) If someone says to a slanderer (qadhif) 'sadaqta' (you have spoken the truth) without specifying what he is affirming, the speaker is not subject to the hadd of qadhf (80 lashes) because the words could mean he is generally truthful, not that the slander is true. (2) If someone makes an indirect reference (ta'rid) to slander, he is also not flogged because ta'rid is a type of kinayah that lacks clarity. The lesson applies the usul definition of kinayah to a fiqh ruling, showing how linguistic ambiguity has tangible legal consequences. Note: The text cuts off mid-sentence, likely continuing with further examples.

Key Terms
قصور ظاهر (quṣūr ẓāhir): Apparent insufficiency of the word to clearly convey the intended meaning.
خفاء صريح (khafā' ṣarīḥ): Clear hiddenness; the ambiguity is manifest and intentional.
شبهة العدم (shubhat al-'adam): Semblance of non-existence of the intended meaning; doubt that what was meant actually occurred.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is a person who says 'sadaqta' (you spoke truth) to a known slanderer (qadhif) not subject to the hadd of qadhf according to this passage?
A) Because 'sadaqta' is a kinayah that could mean general truthfulness, creating doubt about whether the slander was affirmed.
B) Because the person did not repeat the slanderous statement himself.
C) Because the slanderer was not punished first.
D) Because 'sadaqta' is an explicit insult that requires a different punishment.

Key Takeaway
Kinayah's inherent ambiguity creates a shubha that triggers the principle of averting hudud, so no hadd punishment is established by kinayah.

Acknowledgment with 'Lakin' as Rejection or Transfer
Usul rule on the effect of 'lakin' in iqrar

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul principle that 'lakin' when syntactically connected (mawsul) indicates transfer (tahwil) and when disconnected (mafsul) indicates rejection (radd) in cases of iqrar.

Core Idea
In Hanafi iqrar, 'lakin' connected transfers the debt; disconnected rejects the claim.

This lesson explores a nuanced case in Hanafi usul: when a person acknowledges a debt, and the creditor responds with 'it was not mine, but for so-and-so,' the legal effect depends on how 'lakin' is phrased. The Hanafi school uses syntactical connection to distinguish between a rejection of the claim and a transfer of the debt to a third party. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly interpreting acknowledgments in Islamic law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the iqrar scenario and the statement
Examine the acknowledgment: a man says 'so-and-so has one thousand owed to me,' and the acknowledgee says 'it was not mine but for so-and-so.' Note the presence of 'lakin' (but).
2
Classify the syntactical connection of 'lakin'
Determine whether 'lakin' is connected (mawsul) to the preceding sentence or disconnected (mafsul). Connected means there is no pause; the statement is one unit. Disconnected means there is a pause or the sentence is independent.
Hanafi usul attaches legal consequences to this syntactical distinction.
3
Apply the Hanafi principle
If connected, 'lakin' indicates transfer (tahwil): the debt originally belonged to the acknowledgee but is now transferred to the third party. If disconnected, it indicates rejection (radd): the acknowledgee denies ever having the debt, voiding the acknowledgment.
The principle is that 'lakin' when connected changes the meaning of the prior statement; when disconnected, it begins a new independent statement.
4
Consider the qarina and variant from Fakhr al-Islam
The passage notes that when the acknowledgee says 'it was never mine' (using 'qatt'), it precludes transfer because ownership is denied ab initio. Also, if 'lakin' is used as a qarina (contextual evidence), its effect depends on connection.
This shows that the analysis is not purely mechanical; context (qarina) plays a role.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فرع: إذا أقر رجل له علي ألف فقال المقر له ما كان لي لكن لفلان، فحينئذ (قول المقر له ما كان لي لكن لفلان ظاهر في الرد أي رد الإقرار (ويحتمل التحويل) لفلان عليه، فصار هذا الدين أولاً للمقر له ثم صار لفلان بتحويله ولما كان) التحويل (تغييراً) لظاهر الكلام يصح إذا كان قوله لكن لفلان (موصولاً للتوقف أي لتوقف أول الكلام على آخره، وهذا مغير له فيصح موصولاً ولا يصح مفصولاً، لأن بيان التغيير لا ينفصل ولا يتوقف الكلام على ما هو منفصل عنه هذا وفرض الإمام فخر الإسلام المسألة في العبد، وفيه قول المقر له : ما كان لي قط لكن لفلان وعلى هذا لا يصح التحويل بل التقرير الأولى أن كلامه ظاهر في الرد، ويحتمل أن يكون مقصوده أن العبد أو الدين وإن اشتهر أنه عبدي أو ديني لكنه في الحقيقة لفلان فصار بعد قبول إقراره لنفسه إقراره لغيره ولكن لفلان قرينة عليه، فإن كان موصولاً لا يكون رداً وإن كان مفصولاً يتم الرد، ولا يسمع قوله، لكن لفلان فتدبر.
Subsidiary issue: If a man acknowledges, 'So-and-so has one thousand owed to me,' and the person acknowledged to says, 'It was not mine but for so-and-so,' then his saying 'It was not mine but for so-and-so' is apparently a rejection of the acknowledgment. It also may imply a transfer to so-and-so. This debt first belonged to the acknowledgee, then became for so-and-so by transfer. Since transfer changes the apparent meaning, it is valid if his saying 'but for so-and-so' is connected, because the first part depends on the last part, and this changes it. So it is valid when connected, not when disconnected, because the statement of change is not separate, and the speech does not depend on what is separate from it. Imam Fakhr al-Islam considered the issue regarding a slave, where the acknowledgee says, 'It was never mine but for so-and-so.' In this case, transfer is not valid; rather, the primary view is that his statement is apparent in rejection. It is possible that his intent is that the slave or debt, even if reputed to be mine, is in reality for so-and-so. So after accepting his acknowledgment for himself, it becomes an acknowledgment for another, but 'but for so-and-so' is a qarina. If connected, it is not rejection; if disconnected, the rejection is complete, and his saying 'but for so-and-so' is not heard. So ponder.
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage is a Hanafi usul analysis of a specific case of acknowledgment (iqrar) where the creditor says 'it was not mine but for so-and-so.' The key issue is whether 'lakin' (but) is syntactically connected (mawsul) or disconnected (mafsul). If connected, it changes the meaning: the debtor owes the debt to the third party via transfer (tahwil). If disconnected, it acts as a rejection (radd) of the acknowledgment, meaning the debt never belonged to the first person. Hanafi fiqh uses the principle that 'lakin' when used for istidrak (correction) requires connection. The passage also notes a variant from Imam Fakhr al-Islam regarding a slave, where the statement 'I never owned it' precludes transfer because ownership is denied ab initio. The commentary clarifies the Hanafi position on the force of qarina (contextual evidence) in interpreting such statements.

Key Terms
إِقْرَار (iqrār): Acknowledgment of a right or debt by a person against himself; it is binding in Hanafi law.
تَحْوِيل (taḥwīl): Transfer of a debt from one creditor to another, requiring the debtor's consent in Hanafi law.
قَرِينَة (qarīna): Contextual or circumstantial evidence that clarifies the intended meaning of a statement.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the iqrar case, if the acknowledgee says 'it was not mine but for so-and-so' with 'lakin' disconnected (mafsul), what is the ruling?
A) The debt transfers to so-and-so.
B) The acknowledgment is rejected; the debt is not owed.
C) The statement is ineffective.
D) The debtor must pay the acknowledgee.

Key Takeaway
The legal effect of 'lakin' in iqrar depends on syntactical connection: connected indicates transfer (tahwil), disconnected indicates rejection (radd).
